<div id="review_2220227604" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2220227604" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2220227604?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141189383" href="/book/show/6801335-alone-in-berlin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2220227604" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17510408854510007944">Berlin, 1940. While Hitler celebrates conquering France, a working class German couple – Otto and Anna Quangel – mourn the passing of their son, Ottochen, who fell in the fighting. Bitterly upset at the Fuhrer, they begin a quiet campaign of civil disobedience against his Third Reich, dropping hand-made postcards with anti-Nazi slogans printed on them across Berlin. The treasonous postcards are soon noticed and the Gestapo quickly take up the hunt for the culprits – but how long can the Quangels</span>
  <span id="freeText17510408854510007944" style="display:none">Berlin, 1940. While Hitler celebrates conquering France, a working class German couple – Otto and Anna Quangel – mourn the passing of their son, Ottochen, who fell in the fighting. Bitterly upset at the Fuhrer, they begin a quiet campaign of civil disobedience against his Third Reich, dropping hand-made postcards with anti-Nazi slogans printed on them across Berlin. The treasonous postcards are soon noticed and the Gestapo quickly take up the hunt for the culprits – but how long can the Quangels evade capture? <br /><br />German writer Hans Fallada’s novel Alone in Berlin (published in America under the title Every Man Dies Alone) was written in just 24 days in 1946. Fallada (real name Rudolf Ditzen) passed away from heart failure, after decades of drug and alcohol abuse, mere months after completing it, and never saw its publication in 1947. Despite doing well in both East and West Germany in the ensuing years, the book wasn’t published in English until 2009 when it became a surprise bestseller. And it’s easy to see why – Alone in Berlin is an excellent novel! <br /><br />After the war, Fallada was given the Gestapo file on Otto and Elise Hampel and used their brave, but doomed, campaign of low-key opposition as the basis for his novel. Names are changed here and there, as are the way the characters meet their fates, and Fallada uses the novelist’s prerogative to dramatize his narrative, but the main characters are based on real people from the defiant Quangels to the tenacious and formidable Gestapo Inspector Escherich right down to the utterly despicable Nazi Judge Feisler towards the end. <br /><br />It is a very long read at nearly 600 pages and it takes a while to get going, so, if you’re going to give it a shot, be patient with it. I can also understand people’s criticisms about the narrative containing too many tangents. Fallada gives a lot of chapters to a couple of inessential side characters, deadbeats Enno Kluge and Emil Borkhausen, whose stories are only very tenuously connected to the Quangels’. It would definitely be a more streamlined novel had they been eschewed entirely. The same could be said of the story of the disgusting Nazi family, the Persickes, and their black-hearted SS son Baldur. <br /><br />Then again, if you just want the bare facts, go read the Wikipedia entry on the Hampels instead. Like any writer worth his salt, Fallada is more concerned with the art of fiction writing like creating atmosphere, characters, as well as a real feeling and understanding of this insane time to better understand the people involved and their actions. How else to grasp the overblown reaction to the Quangels’ small, almost quaint, postcards, upon which were written simplistic things along the lines of “Hitler is a liar!”, than to experience the fearful and oppressive atmosphere of life than through a sampling of its society? Also, the myriad stories were largely entertaining and I was rarely bored with anything I was reading, regardless of relevance to the primary plotline. <br /><br />More importantly the wider cast serves as a reminder that there were many German people who secretly hated and resisted Hitler and his war and that the Nazis were not representative of the country as a whole – as a Brit, I get the impression that all Germans from this time get unfairly tarred with the same brush. Those not in concentration camps could still easily become victims and prisoners themselves thanks to horribly corrupt and brutal institutions like the Gestapo making their everyday lives hell. <br /><br />All of which sounds like a thoroughly depressing read, no? It is and it isn’t. Because, though Fallada treats the subject matter with the utmost respect - not to mention the incidence of characters committing suicide, getting murdered, suffering miscarriages, and being executed! - it’s never overwhelmingly grim, melodramatic or sentimental so it doesn’t wear you down emotionally too much. Quite often it weirdly reads like a thriller and, after the initial slow start, it really picks up steam the deeper into it you go; as odd as it sounds given all that, I found it a very enjoyable read. <br /><br />Fallada was undeniably a talented writer and, though his character portraits of certain characters – particularly the Nazis – were one-dimensional, he importantly gives the main ones necessary nuance to stand out. Gestapo Inspector Escherich is an especially memorable and imposing figure whose own fate came as a shock - he strongly reminded me of Christoph Waltz’s Hans Landa from Inglourious Basterds. <br /><br />It has some fat to it that might do with some selective editing as some passages (the interrogation scenes) are a bit dull and slow but on the whole Alone in Berlin is a compelling and exciting novel that paints a vivid and startling image of life in wartime Germany at the very heart of the Third Reich. As well as cementing his own literary legacy with this book, Fallada ensured the courageous sacrifices of Otto and Elise Hampel live on through the years to be discovered by new generations of readers - a powerful story that deserves to be remembered. Alone in Berlin is a remarkable novel I’m glad to have read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17510408854510007944" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_143190430" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143190430" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2569035-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455213348p2/2569035.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/143190430?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/2569035-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141189383" href="/book/show/6801335-alone-in-berlin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2569035-mark?shelf=bookclub-reads">bookclub-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2569035-mark?shelf=war-works">war-works</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143190430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8357298663164362345">After having started 2011 with a couple of disappointing novels this one blew me away. Written in 1947 but set in the middle years of the war it follows a number of different characters ranging from the noble and kind through the naive and tragic to the utterly loathsome making a few stops at the fairly disgusting. All emotions are here and this reader certainly experienced quite a few of them himself. <br/><br/>The hero and heroine,(Fallada speaks of people in their fifties or even late forties as being </span>
  <span id="freeText8357298663164362345" style="display:none">After having started 2011 with a couple of disappointing novels this one blew me away. Written in 1947 but set in the middle years of the war it follows a number of different characters ranging from the noble and kind through the naive and tragic to the utterly loathsome making a few stops at the fairly disgusting. All emotions are here and this reader certainly experienced quite a few of them himself. <br /><br />The hero and heroine,(Fallada speaks of people in their fifties or even late forties as being old,... this is quite sobering for a man careering towards his 50's but because of the power of this book i'll let it pass !!)an older married couple, having initially been supporters or at least not opponents of the regime in Germany are thrust into a space by the death of their only Son which forces them to reconsider and so begins their simple rebellion of writing annonymous postcards against the Nazis and leaving them in public places. <br /><br />The story is about their courage and struggles and the way their actions affect or involve others. There are side stories involving various other berliners, many of whom, without knowing it, live side by side with these unnoticed and unremembered rebels. The novel delves deep into the horror and injustice present I suppose in all oppressive and cruel dehumanizing regimes but Otto and Anna's refusal to be cowed is amazingly moving. <br /><br />There is even humour; the chapters in the court room would be ridiculous and farcical if it wasn't for the verdicts at the end but the defendants' responses are magnificently powerful. There is an enormous number of brutal and cruel deaths here but nobody reading a book set in Germany in 1942 can be surprised at that and yet the surprise for me is the fact that I was left feeling positive.<br /><br /> <br />There was horror, cruelty and monstrous injustice but the ability of love to overcome betrayal, rejection and seeming hopelessness are writ large here. The book revolves around relationships; the life giving sort of men and women in love, the positive affect that can be brought about in a life by the unconditional offering of acceptance, the renewal and recharging of hope because someone loves you and then on the negative side the opposite. How brutalized and rejected people can come to lose all human sympathy and begin to relate to others only as sources of income, entertainment or revenge. <br /><br />The over riding relationship in the background is perhaps that of parent to child. We see good parents mourning the death of a son, we see a good parent being devestated by the realization of her child's brutality, we see a longed for child lost, we see a nigh on demonic relationship between a son and a father who appear to have lost all sense of humanity and we see a brutalized child escaping to new hope with a new parent. Looking back i have used 'brutal' a lot, I apologize for that lack of vocabulary but i suppose that is my over riding image of the book but underpinning it is that other recurring word, love. Do read this book. it's extraordinary.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8357298663164362345" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1696812914" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1696812914" />
    <a title="Book Riot Community" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33447134-book-riot-community"><img alt="Book Riot Community" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583162430p2/33447134.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1696812914?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Book Riot Community" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Book Riot Community" href="/user/show/33447134-book-riot-community">Book Riot Community</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1696812914" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17348387187568927243">This is an absolutely devastating novel about Otto and Anna Quangel, an older working-class German couple during World War II who wrote anti-Nazi messages on postcards and left them around Berlin. The two work on their own, not part of any larger resistance movement, and they have no way of knowing whether their messages are having any effect. It gets off to a slow start, as the Quangels start their work and the authorities begin to take notice and sift through various suspects to find the culpr</span>
  <span id="freeText17348387187568927243" style="display:none">This is an absolutely devastating novel about Otto and Anna Quangel, an older working-class German couple during World War II who wrote anti-Nazi messages on postcards and left them around Berlin. The two work on their own, not part of any larger resistance movement, and they have no way of knowing whether their messages are having any effect. It gets off to a slow start, as the Quangels start their work and the authorities begin to take notice and sift through various suspects to find the culprits. But the later chapters, as the Quangels explain their actions and decide how to deal with the consequences are terrifying and wrenching. Based on the true story of Otto and Elise Hampel and first published in 1947, this book sends a powerful message about the importance of speaking up for good, regardless of the results.<br /><br />-Teresa Preston<br /><br /><br />from The Best Books We Read In June 2016: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2016/06/29/riot-round-up-the-best-books-we-read-in-june-3/">http://bookriot.com/2016/06/29/riot-r...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17348387187568927243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_194491818" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194491818" />
    <a title="Cara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2744176-cara"><img alt="Cara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1253106104p2/2744176.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194491818?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cara" href="/user/show/2744176-cara">Cara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141189383" href="/book/show/6801335-alone-in-berlin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2744176-cara?shelf=eccleston-reading-group">eccleston-reading-group</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2744176-cara?shelf=read-2011">read-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2744176-cara?shelf=holiday-read">holiday-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194491818" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14055006658110079900">If I could have given this six stars, I would have. <br/><br/>Maybe it was because I read it in a day, or maybe because it was based on a true story, I know I will not forget this book for a long time.<br/><br/>Much WW2 literature is written from the view point of the English during the blitz, the French heading up the Resistence or the Nazi's wreaking evil. I think there is only Alone in Berlin and The Book Thief that I have read, which has given an insight into the dire situation that the ordinary Germans lived t</span>
  <span id="freeText14055006658110079900" style="display:none">If I could have given this six stars, I would have. <br /><br />Maybe it was because I read it in a day, or maybe because it was based on a true story, I know I will not forget this book for a long time.<br /><br />Much WW2 literature is written from the view point of the English during the blitz, the French heading up the Resistence or the Nazi's wreaking evil. I think there is only Alone in Berlin and The Book Thief that I have read, which has given an insight into the dire situation that the ordinary Germans lived through to survive the War. <br /><br />The loss of their son fighting for Hitler, sets Otto &amp; Anna on a path that once started upon, they cannot stop. It may seem a very trivial or weak way to fight out against the Fuhrer, by leaving postcards with anti-Nazi propoganda written upon them all around Berlin. It is however, an act or mission which they will pay for with their lives if caught.<br /><br />The other characters who play out alongside Otto &amp; Anna are all brilliantly drawn. Defined by their bravery, evilness, cunning or fecklesness. None of them are superfluous to the story, even old Judge Fromm makes his final appearance worthy of his strange actions at the beginning.<br /><br />Other factors which made this book amazing for me alongside the fact that it was closely based on a true story, is that it was written in only twenty four days and was first released in 1947. It reads like a modern day thriller and the detail only serves to heighten the suspense and fear you have for the characters. A hefty volume, but definitely worth the investment of your time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14055006658110079900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_77293050" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77293050" />
    <a title="Whitaker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker"><img alt="Whitaker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410926609p2/1415047.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/77293050?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whitaker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whitaker" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker">Whitaker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=germany">germany</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=z_2009-read">z_2009-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=fiction-1900-1950">fiction-1900-1950</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer77293050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17571587795630934318">I read this while I was also reading Robert Fisk's <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52142.The_Great_War_for_Civilisation_The_Conquest_of_the_Middle_East' title='The Great War for Civilisation The Conquest of the Middle East by Robert Fisk' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Great War for Civilisation The Conquest of the Middle East</a>
</i>. Bad idea. Very bad idea. Note to self: Reading two depressing books at the same time does not do good things to one's mood. <br/><br/>There has been a surge of interest in the German experience of World War II, particularly the experience of those who tried to resist the war mongering. This novel joins works like <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/742642.The_Song_Before_It_Is_Sung_A_Novel' title='The Song Before It Is Sung A Novel by Justin Cartwright' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Song Before It Is Sung A Novel</a>
</i>, <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6251003.Valkyrie_The_Plot_To_Kill_Hitler' title='Valkyrie The Plot To Kill Hitler by Philipp von Boeselager' rel='nofollow noopener'>Valkyrie The Plot To Kill Hitler</a>
</i></span>
  <span id="freeText17571587795630934318" style="display:none">I read this while I was also reading Robert Fisk's <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52142.The_Great_War_for_Civilisation_The_Conquest_of_the_Middle_East" title="The Great War for Civilisation The Conquest of the Middle East by Robert Fisk" rel="nofollow noopener">The Great War for Civilisation The Conquest of the Middle East</a>
</i>. Bad idea. Very bad idea. Note to self: Reading two depressing books at the same time does not do good things to one's mood. <br /><br />There has been a surge of interest in the German experience of World War II, particularly the experience of those who tried to resist the war mongering. This novel joins works like <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/742642.The_Song_Before_It_Is_Sung_A_Novel" title="The Song Before It Is Sung A Novel by Justin Cartwright" rel="nofollow noopener">The Song Before It Is Sung A Novel</a>
</i>, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6251003.Valkyrie_The_Plot_To_Kill_Hitler" title="Valkyrie The Plot To Kill Hitler by Philipp von Boeselager" rel="nofollow noopener">Valkyrie The Plot To Kill Hitler</a>
</i>, and <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6093585.Sophie_Scholl_The_Real_Story_Behind_German_s_Resistance_Heroine" title="Sophie Scholl The Real Story Behind German's Resistance Heroine by Frank McDonough" rel="nofollow noopener">Sophie Scholl The Real Story Behind German's Resistance Heroine</a>
</i>. It was, however, written long before, in 1947 by Hans Fallada, and in that sense, can be regarded as almost a synchronous record. I am glad for this rehabilitation. It is always useful to remember that no group anywhere is a monolithic bloc and in this age of a resurgent right and rising Islamophobia, it is a lesson that is only too important to remember. <br /><br />What makes this book, for me, less a four or five star effort was its treatment of its characters. Good or bad fell into more or less easily defined camps. The Nazis were brutal bullies; the resisters were good hearted but ineffectual. <br /><br />I don't think life is that simple. The story touched on but did not delve into the fact that most people are just scared. Scared of being denounced, scared of dying, it's so much easier to just go along, to close your eyes to the horror around you. How many of us would do that? I'm pretty sure I would cave, especially if faced by torture and the deaths of my nearest and dearest. <br /><br />Worse. How many of us would be immune to <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7170183.The_Lucifer_Effect" title="The Lucifer Effect by Philip Zimbardo" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lucifer Effect</a>
</i>, that infamous Berkeley experiment where ordinary men off the street turned into brutal thugs when placed in charge of another group of men off the street and told to treat them like prisoners? The craven eager willingness to give in to calls to invade Iraq gives me little hope: when even in the "land of the free and the brave" dissenting voices squelch their nagging doubts what are the chances that we'll be better than that? <br /><br />What I liked about the book was its portrayal of ordinary people, holding fast to their beliefs. I'd like to believe that it's possible. I also liked how it raised the eternal question: what form should resistance take? Is it still necessary even if or even <u>when</u> it's utterly useless? This book says, and says resoundingly, "Yes!" <br /><br />I want to believe that. I really do. Oh, but how easy it would be to rationalize collaboration, to say, "I can do more good subverting the system from within."</span>
  <a data-text-id="17571587795630934318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_40019175" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40019175" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40019175?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781935554042" href="/book/show/6921128-every-man-dies-alone">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=dnf">dnf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=kirkus">kirkus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=swap">swap</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=bio">bio</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40019175" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8625412079645768212">Having read through 185 pages and disliking every minute spent with the book, I am stopping. All of my criticisms remain. Fallada wrote this book in 24 days. It shows. IF SOMEONE WANTS TO READ THIS BOOK - CONTACT ME, MAYBE WE CAN SWAP bOOKS!<br/><br/>P.S. I went back and reread the Kirkus review. I should have read the review more carefully. It is clearly stated that the characters are "archetypal to a fault". I recommend that carefully read Kirkus's review. Here follows a link to that review: <br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://search.barnesandnoble.com/books/product.aspx?r=1&ISBN=1935554042&r=1'>http://sear</a></span>
  <span id="freeText8625412079645768212" style="display:none">Having read through 185 pages and disliking every minute spent with the book, I am stopping. All of my criticisms remain. Fallada wrote this book in 24 days. It shows. IF SOMEONE WANTS TO READ THIS BOOK - CONTACT ME, MAYBE WE CAN SWAP bOOKS!<br /><br />P.S. I went back and reread the Kirkus review. I should have read the review more carefully. It is clearly stated that the characters are "archetypal to a fault". I recommend that carefully read Kirkus's review. Here follows a link to that review: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://search.barnesandnoble.com/books/product.aspx?r=1&amp;ISBN=1935554042&amp;r=1">http://search.barnesandnoble.com/book...</a>. It is found at the bottom of the page.<br /><br />I am on page 172 and I thought I just cannot stand reading more of this book...... then I went and looked at the book description and noted that 22 of my GR friends have this on the to-read shelves. Of the four of my friends who have read the book, one gave it 5 stars and three others gave it 4 stars. The average rating is 4.11 with 728 people having read the book! And I really hate it. Now I am thinking I simply must finsih reding it to give a complete report of my views. If I stop now, I haven't given the book a fair chance. So I will continue but the following is what I am currently thinking. <br /><br />Nobody can say that his author has a way with words. The writing is just plain ordinary. The characters are primarily despicable, so it is logical that their language is too. I must accept that. However I do not believe that despicable people have to be described by means of a flat text. Their is no sarcasm. There is no humor. There is no irony. The text is just plain flat.<br /><br />Fallada want to draw a picture of the fear that dragged all Germans down under Hitler's regime. We are to understand how the German people suffered too. I have no problem with that; they too suffered under Hitler. BUT there is NO discussion whatsoever about how originally the Germans in fact looked at Hitler as a person who would bring order to their life and economy. There is not a hint of this in the text. <br /><br />The primary couple in this book are trying to revolt in their own little way, and of course that is good, but but I thoroughly dislike the brutish way in which the huge majority of Germans are depicted. Some of these Germans were moral, good, just people. Other than the couple, I see none of these in this book. Every German official is depicted as a dumb clout. There is one jurist who tries to help a Jew and even he is drawn in negative contours. The fear prevalent in the German socity is made very clear. Most people didn't have the strength to fight this, but some did. <br /><br />So what am I saying? I am saying that the picture drawn of the German population is done without insight or nuance. That is what I think now. I will continue reading.<br /><br />Through page 86: What isit with me? This book has gotten rave reviews, and me - I keep falling asleep when I read it! I don't feel for any of the main protagonists and the writing is just plain ordinary. I hope it gets better.<br /><br />And here is a quote from page 86 to show you what I mean:<br /><br />"While Enno is trotting around the streets, timidly looking for his Tutti, Borkhausen has got up from his bed, gone to the kitchen, and savagely and broodingly eats his fill. Then Borkhausen finds a pack of cigarettes in the wardrobe, slips it in his pocket, and sits down at the table again, pondering gloomily head in hand."<br /><br />"Which is how Otti finds him when she returns from the shop. Of course she sees right away that he's helped himself to some food, and she knows he didn't have any smokes on him and traces the theft to her wardrobe. Apprehensive as she is, she starts an argument right away. 'Yes, that's my darling, a man who eats my food and snitches my cigarettes! Give them back right now. Or pay me back for them. Give me some money, Emil.'"<br /><br />Boring!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8625412079645768212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_106907765" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106907765" />
    <a title="Esteban del Mal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2251009-esteban-del-mal"><img alt="Esteban del Mal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432140681p2/2251009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/106907765?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Esteban del Mal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Esteban del Mal" href="/user/show/2251009-esteban-del-mal">Esteban del Mal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781935554042" href="/book/show/6921128-every-man-dies-alone">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2251009-esteban-del-mal?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2251009-esteban-del-mal?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2251009-esteban-del-mal?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer106907765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17749837548860897861">Nazis: history's equivalent to that team that always gets trounced by the Harlem Globetrotters, the Washington Generals. <br/><br/>Every time you see Nazis in a movie or read of Nazis in book, you know that they're gonna get theirs in the end. It's akin to something like culturally accepted wisdom to dismiss them as caricatures. But they aren't caricatures (Godwin's Law notwithstanding) -- they existed (DO exist), and for a while there it looked like they might even run things. The period of their ascenda</span>
  <span id="freeText17749837548860897861" style="display:none">Nazis: history's equivalent to that team that always gets trounced by the Harlem Globetrotters, the Washington Generals. <br /><br />Every time you see Nazis in a movie or read of Nazis in book, you know that they're gonna get theirs in the end. It's akin to something like culturally accepted wisdom to dismiss them as caricatures. But they aren't caricatures (Godwin's Law notwithstanding) -- they existed (DO exist), and for a while there it looked like they might even run things. The period of their ascendancy can hardly be over-examined, because we can't afford anything even remotely resembling it coming about again.<br /><br />Hans Fallada (the nom de plume of Rudolf Ditzen) wrote this book in an astonishing 24 days (about the same amount of time it took me to read it -- OY!) upon being released from an insane asylum after the German surrender in WWII. He did not live to see his novel published. I can't even begin to imagine what he experienced. You can see flashes of his talent throughout, but the whole lacks a certain consistency.<br /><br />The story is a fictionalized account of the true story of a working class couple that distributed postcards anonymously throughout Berlin urging Germans to revolt, sabotage and generally undermine the Nazis whenever possible. The couple attached great importance to the postcards, believing they were having the desired effect on the populace. Of course the postcards were generally reviled and went unheeded, only holding significance to their authors (think all of us here on Goodreads). Yet even when the couple is captured, learn that their postcard campaign accomplished nothing, and they are condemned to death, Falluda captures their dignity. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17749837548860897861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6554815358485828568">Arendt wrote of the 'banality of evil.' Fallada's book is maybe about the futility of good, or the absence of cohesion amongst those who were not endangered directly. <br/><br/>This WW2 story concerns non-Semitic Germans during this period. It melts into a pond of existentialism and it bespeaks another aspect of imperfect humanity.</span>

<div id="review_711411284" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/711411284" />
    <a title="booklady" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/350218-booklady"><img alt="booklady" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591723471p2/350218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/711411284?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="booklady" class="user" itemprop="url" name="booklady" href="/user/show/350218-booklady">booklady</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/350218-booklady?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer711411284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3666914599131742040">October 31, 2016: Just learned from my friend, Kerstin, there is now a movie about this book, <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=S0mPmu6cTIo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alone in Berlin</a>. I am not surprised. This is an amazing book. Dark, DARK, <b>Dark</b>, but so appropriate for this age we are living in ... preparatory, prophetic. Into our culture of death which teaches that nothing really matters comes a book and film which teaches the opposite; even our smallest little protests against this grinding machine COUNT. We count. Each and every one of us, from the very smallest u</span>
  <span id="freeText3666914599131742040" style="display:none">October 31, 2016: Just learned from my friend, Kerstin, there is now a movie about this book, <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=S0mPmu6cTIo" rel="nofollow noopener">Alone in Berlin</a>. I am not surprised. This is an amazing book. Dark, DARK, <b>Dark</b>, but so appropriate for this age we are living in ... preparatory, prophetic. Into our culture of death which teaches that nothing really matters comes a book and film which teaches the opposite; even our smallest little protests against this grinding machine COUNT. We count. Each and every one of us, from the very smallest unwanted embryo at the beginning of life to the forgotten soul clinging to life in some prison hole or hospital. We are all precious and every single thing we do, every hair on our head, cell of our bodies, though it may <i>seem</i> alone and forgotten is not. On the contrary, we are held dear beyond our wildest imaginings by Him Who IS. <br /><br />Sep 04, 2013: Hans Fallada’s <i>Every Man Dies Alone</i> is the fictionalized account of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otto_and_Elise_Hampel" rel="nofollow noopener">Otto and Elise Hampel</a>’s unsuccessful localized propaganda effort against the Third Reich during World War II. When the author, Fallada first read about the Hampels’ unspectacular and unsophisticated ‘postcard campaign’ immediately after the war, he was not impressed. Of the 287 hand-written and badly spelled cards, 265 were immediately turned into the Nazi authorities. Compared to (say) <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claus_von_Stauffenberg" rel="nofollow noopener">von Stauffenberg</a> and his associates’ attempted <i>coup d’état</i> in July 1944, or ... the literate and cultivated leaflets written by the university-educated dissidents of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_Rose" rel="nofollow noopener">“White Rose”</a> group in 1942-43, their story was singularly uninspiring. And yet, Fallada's understated telling of the Hampels’ quiet, miserable, even hopeless stand against the 20th Century Killing Machine is compelling.<br /><br />Near the end of the book we are given the reason for everything (including the book's name) by an afterthought character, a prison cellmate of Otto, an orchestra director, who explains to Quangel (the fictional name of Otto Hampel) that his actions far from being futile were very meaningful, <i>“As it was, we all acted alone, we were caught alone, and everyone of us will have to die alone. But that doesn’t mean we are alone, Quangel or that our deaths are in vain. Nothing in this world is done in vain, and since we are fighting for justice against brutality, we are bound to prevail in the end.” </i> <br /><br />Fallada was the nom de plume for Rudolf Ditzen, a man who was a composite of good and bad, sometimes working with and sometimes working against ‘the system’ – like his fictional and fictionalized characters. <br /><br /><i>Every Man Dies Alone</i> is one of the most challenging books I’ve read in a long time, which explains why I offered the life raft of the quote above. It is an uphill read as you are fighting evil angry oppression alongside fearful individuals, turned against each other by circumstance, personality and weakness. In addition to the Quangels’, Fallada has created/woven a cast of other characters, some real/altered and some invented: their dead son’s former girlfriend and her associates; residents of their apartment complex; the inspectors investigating their case; family members, suspects, business associates and finally those met in prison. Fleshing out the novel adds interest but also seems to multiply despair … at least initially.<br /><br />Amazing story and yes, <i>‘nothing in the world is done in vain’</i>! <br /><br /> <br /><br />======================================<br />Finished it late last night or early this a.m. I'm so glad I stuck with it, although I was wondering. Review to follow. Need some time...<br /><br />Have been stopping and starting this book, reading other lighter books in between. Not sure if that is 'cheating' or helping me some perspective...? </span>
  <a data-text-id="3666914599131742040" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_70817767" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/70817767" />
    <a title="Meaghan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/430758-meaghan"><img alt="Meaghan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584945127p2/430758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/70817767?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meaghan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meaghan" href="/user/show/430758-meaghan">Meaghan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/430758-meaghan?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/430758-meaghan?shelf=adult-novels">adult-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/430758-meaghan?shelf=worldwar2">worldwar2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/430758-meaghan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70817767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5505672068637143247">This has got to be the best book I've read in months, at least. Certainly the best novel. I had been waiting for it for months (the library had only one copy and others were ahead of me), and it was worth it. I sat down and read the whole book in a single day.<br/><br/>The premise is excellent -- a perfectly ordinary, working-class German couple carries on their own private campaign of resistance by dropping postcards with anti-Nazi messages. I knew this was going to be a great story. But even more impres</span>
  <span id="freeText5505672068637143247" style="display:none">This has got to be the best book I've read in months, at least. Certainly the best novel. I had been waiting for it for months (the library had only one copy and others were ahead of me), and it was worth it. I sat down and read the whole book in a single day.<br /><br />The premise is excellent -- a perfectly ordinary, working-class German couple carries on their own private campaign of resistance by dropping postcards with anti-Nazi messages. I knew this was going to be a great story. But even more impressive was the author's characterization. He has the ability to make the most minor characters seem real, and altogether human -- there are no heroes in this book, not even among the resisters. And the book has many characters and many storylines all going on at once, but Fallada never once seems to lose track of anything and all the plot threads are woven seamlessly together.<br /><br />The afterword tells of Fallada's life (basically one disaster after another) and of the real-life couple who inspired the book. It was a useful addition, but the story can stand on its own.<br /><br />All I can say is: WOW. I will definitely recommend this book to all my friends.<br /><br />I wrote about the real-life people behind this novel, Otto and Elise Hampel, in one of my many <a href="http://www.executedtoday.com/2011/04/08/1943-elise-and-otto-hampel-postcard-writers/" rel="nofollow noopener">guest entries</a> on the blog Executed Today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5505672068637143247" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_771931702" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/771931702" />
    <a title="Paola" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8934711-paola"><img alt="Paola" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375823723p2/8934711.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/771931702?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paola" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paola" href="/user/show/8934711-paola">Paola</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8934711-paola?shelf=wwii">wwii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8934711-paola?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer771931702" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8432082570878858066">This is a beautiful book painting the lives of common people living through the terrible years of Nazi Germany, written just after the war by somebody who actually lived in lived through those years.<br/><br/>Fallada introduces us to carpenter Otto Quangel and his stay at home wife Anna in the first chapter, and we stay with these main protagonists for the whole book - but all around them many diverse specimens of humanity help paint what it was like. None of these characters is flat or stereotypical: eve</span>
  <span id="freeText8432082570878858066" style="display:none">This is a beautiful book painting the lives of common people living through the terrible years of Nazi Germany, written just after the war by somebody who actually lived in lived through those years.<br /><br />Fallada introduces us to carpenter Otto Quangel and his stay at home wife Anna in the first chapter, and we stay with these main protagonists for the whole book - but all around them many diverse specimens of humanity help paint what it was like. None of these characters is flat or stereotypical: even for the more unidimensional among them we do get to see how they perceive themselves and justify their own actions.<br /><br />Some of the chapters are painful to go through: the Gestapo interrogations (especially those by inspector Laub), the trials of the People's Court are torturing. Some of these descriptions seem out of proportion, then you think that Fallada himself experienced interrogations and prison, and it gets more blood curling. As one would expect, we see depicted the struggle between the pure evil of Nazism and the fundamental decency of those who could simply not debase themselves, that could not be satisfied with trying to get by since nobody can win on his own. A stubborness in dignity, summarised in an exchange between Quangel and and Sr. Richard:<br /><blockquote>Would you rather live for an un­just cause than die for a just one? There is no choice—not for you, nor for me ei­ther. It’s be­cause we are as we are that we have to go this way</blockquote><br /><br />In spite of this I find myself disagreeing with <a href="http://researchers.uq.edu.au/researcher/490" rel="nofollow noopener">Geoff Wilkes</a> in the afterword, when he writes that "whereas Hanna Aredt's <i>Eichmann in Jerusalem</i> (1936) dissects and analyzes "the banality of evil", Hans Fallada's <i>Every Man Dies Alone</i> comprehends and honors the banality of good". True, some of the actions of passive resistance (which often translated in a death sentence) are born of a set of accidental circumstances and then metamorphose in stronger acts of defiance almost on their own, as if extra cogs thrown into a mechanism were somehow slotted into place. And yes, once these ordinary people cross the path of the Gestapo, nothing much can help them (as is the case of Enno Kluge, Trudel and Karl). But these people, who lived in absolute terror of putting a foot wrong unwittingly, were very aware of the terrible consequences of even the meekest act of resistance: crossing knowingly the threshold of the 'unlawful' required a great deal of courage.<br /><br />Having read this book without knowing anything about Fallada, couple of times I found him heavy handed, excessive somehow in uderscoring the point just made: for instance, after a prisoner commits suicide while in the care of a priest <blockquote>In con­se­quence of this sui­cide, it was the prison chap­lain, Friedrich Lorenz, who was sus­pended from duty, rather than the drunken doc­tor. Charges were laid against the priest. Be­cause it was a crime and the abet­ting of a crime to en­able a pris­oner to put an end to his own life: only the state and its ser­vants were sup­posed to have that pre­rog­a­tive.</blockquote><br />This is clear enough, then Fallada feels the need to add <blockquote>If a de­tec­tive pis­tol-whips a man so badly that his skull is frac­tured, and if a drunken doc­tor al­lows the in­jured man to die, both are an ex­am­ple of due process. Whereas if a priest fails to hin­der a sui­cide, if he al­lows a pris­oner to ex­er­cise his or her will—that will is sup­posed to have been taken away—then he has com­mit­ted a crime and must be pun­ished.</blockquote><br />As a piece of literature, this second paragraph seems unnecessary. But considering this novel was written in about a month, just over one year and a half after the fall of Hitler, this is just raw rage from a survivor.<br /><br />It is a beautifully written book, and one that will stay with me for a long time.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8432082570878858066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_879703934" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/879703934" />
    <a title="Nigeyb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10301421-nigeyb"><img alt="Nigeyb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593065725p2/10301421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/879703934?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nigeyb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nigeyb" href="/user/show/10301421-nigeyb">Nigeyb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781846140822" href="/book/show/6296887-alone-in-berlin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer879703934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4907779314860418042"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/227026.Hans_Fallada' title='Hans Fallada' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hans Fallada</a> was all but forgotten outside Germany when this 1947 novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6801335.Alone_in_Berlin' title='Alone in Berlin by Hans Fallada' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alone in Berlin</a> (US title: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3344411.Every_Man_Dies_Alone' title='Every Man Dies Alone by Hans Fallada' rel='nofollow noopener'>Every Man Dies Alone</a>), was reissued in English in 2009, whereupon it became a best seller and reintroduced <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/227026.Hans_Fallada' title='Hans Fallada' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hans Fallada</a>'s work to a new generation of readers. <br/><br/>I came to this book having read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13503644.More_Lives_Than_One_A_Biography_of_Hans_Fallada' title='More Lives Than One A Biography of Hans Fallada by Jenny Williams' rel='nofollow noopener'>More Lives Than One: A Biography of Hans Fallada</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/107889.Jenny_Williams' title='Jenny Williams' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jenny Williams</a>, which was the perfect introduction into the literary world of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/227026.Hans_Fallada' title='Hans Fallada' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hans Fallada</a>. <br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6296887.Alone_in_Berlin' title='Alone in Berlin by Hans Fallada' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alone In Berlin</a> really brings alive the day-to-day hell of life u</span>
  <span id="freeText4907779314860418042" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/227026.Hans_Fallada" title="Hans Fallada" rel="nofollow noopener">Hans Fallada</a> was all but forgotten outside Germany when this 1947 novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6801335.Alone_in_Berlin" title="Alone in Berlin by Hans Fallada" rel="nofollow noopener">Alone in Berlin</a> (US title: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3344411.Every_Man_Dies_Alone" title="Every Man Dies Alone by Hans Fallada" rel="nofollow noopener">Every Man Dies Alone</a>), was reissued in English in 2009, whereupon it became a best seller and reintroduced <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/227026.Hans_Fallada" title="Hans Fallada" rel="nofollow noopener">Hans Fallada</a>'s work to a new generation of readers. <br /><br />I came to this book having read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13503644.More_Lives_Than_One_A_Biography_of_Hans_Fallada" title="More Lives Than One A Biography of Hans Fallada by Jenny Williams" rel="nofollow noopener">More Lives Than One: A Biography of Hans Fallada</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/107889.Jenny_Williams" title="Jenny Williams" rel="nofollow noopener">Jenny Williams</a>, which was the perfect introduction into the literary world of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/227026.Hans_Fallada" title="Hans Fallada" rel="nofollow noopener">Hans Fallada</a>. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6296887.Alone_in_Berlin" title="Alone in Berlin by Hans Fallada" rel="nofollow noopener">Alone In Berlin</a> really brings alive the day-to-day hell of life under the Nazis - and the ways in which people either compromised their integrity by accepting the regime, or, in some cases, resisted. The insights into life inside Nazi Germany are both fascinating and appalling. The venom of Nazism seeping into every aspect of society leaving no part of daily existence untouched or uncorrupted.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6296887.Alone_in_Berlin" title="Alone in Berlin by Hans Fallada" rel="nofollow noopener">Alone In Berlin</a> is also a thriller, and the tension starts from the first page and mounts with each passing chapter. I can only echo the praise that has been heaped on this astonishingly good, rediscovered World War Two masterpiece. It's a truly great book: gripping, profound and essential.<br /><br />5/5<br /><br />POSTSCRIPT (3 July 2017)...<br /><br />I've just discovered that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3344411.Every_Man_Dies_Alone" title="Every Man Dies Alone by Hans Fallada" rel="nofollow noopener">Every Man Dies Alone</a> (USA title) / <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6801335.Alone_in_Berlin" title="Alone in Berlin by Hans Fallada" rel="nofollow noopener">Alone in Berlin</a> has been made into a film....<br /><br /><i><b>Alone in Berlin</b> is a 2016 war drama film directed by Vincent Pérez and written by Pérez and Achim von Borries, based on the 1947 fictionalized novel Every Man Dies Alone by Hans Fallada. The novel's characters Otto and Anna Quangel are based on the real lives of Otto and Elise Hampel. When their son dies in France, the couple start writing postcards to urge people to protest against Adolf Hitler and the Nazi regime. The film stars Emma Thompson, Brendan Gleeson, and Daniel Brühl. Principal photography began on 27 March 2015 in Berlin. It was selected to compete for the Golden Bear at the 66th Berlin International Film Festival.</i><br /><br />It's just been released in the UK and, according to one of my favourite critics, Mark Kermode, it's pretty damn fine too....<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p057f25p">http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p057f25p</a><br /><br />Any film that features Emma Thompson and Brendan Gleeson automatically has quite a lot to recommend it. I look forward to seeing it given how much I loved the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4907779314860418042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2139355294" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2139355294" />
    <a title="Meike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59050228-meike"><img alt="Meike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514995700p2/59050228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2139355294?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meike" href="/user/show/59050228-meike">Meike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/36329014-jeder-stirbt-f-r-sich-allein">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=2017-read">2017-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=germany">germany</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2139355294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15086936633687232835">(Available in English as "Alone in Berlin" - a truly stupid title, the German original "Everyone dies on his own" is much more apt.)<br/>Fallada wrote this text in only four weeks while he was treated in the psychiatric ward of the Charité Berlin; around two months later, in February 1947, he died of his addiction to morphine and alcoholism (remarkably, the novel features two doctors who try to numb themselves with morphine because they are harassed by the Nazis). "Jeder stirbt für sich allein" is su</span>
  <span id="freeText15086936633687232835" style="display:none">(Available in English as "Alone in Berlin" - a truly stupid title, the German original "Everyone dies on his own" is much more apt.)<br />Fallada wrote this text in only four weeks while he was treated in the psychiatric ward of the Charité Berlin; around two months later, in February 1947, he died of his addiction to morphine and alcoholism (remarkably, the novel features two doctors who try to numb themselves with morphine because they are harassed by the Nazis). "Jeder stirbt für sich allein" is supposed to be the first novel about the resistance against the Third Reich that was written by a German writer who did not emigrate during the Nazi regime.<br /><br />And this is the story Fallada tells: When Otto and Anna Quangel, a working class couple from Berlin, lose their only son on the battlefield, they decide to stand up against the Nazi regime, although their means are limited: They start to secretly write and distribute postcards with anti-fascist messages (a couple like this really existed: Otto and Elise Hampel were executed in 1943). Around this core story, Fallada opens up a panorama of Berlin citizens during WW II: The destiny of a Jewish couple, factory workers who help to keep the war machine going, families who lose their sons and women who lose their husbands, neighbors who spy on each other, poor children and kids who make it to elite Nazi schools (the infamous Napolas), the power of the Gestapo (secret state police), violence and greed that reign freely once the rule of law is abolished - and a climate of constant, all-encompassing fear. In a clever narrative twist, the house in which Otto and Anna live becomes a miniature version of the Third Reich, violently showing the disgusting dynamics behind the whole state. <br /><br />Fallada employs a very simple language, so the book and his message can easily be understood by everyone. His characters are not well-rounded, but rather types. Nevertheless, the psychological dynamics he displays are highly convincing. Fallada's language is most impressive when he opposes the typical Berlin dialect to the language patterns the Nazis employed (both are easily recognisable for every German reader). Smoothly, the author shifts between an omniscient perspective to the point of view of individual characters, so while some parts of the texts are clearly a little lengthy, these shifts help to keep the reader's attention. <br /><br />I did not see the 2016 movie with Emma Thompson that also featured Daniel Brühl (why is he so popular abroad? That guy is the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/163635.Daniel_Kehlmann" title="Daniel Kehlmann" rel="nofollow noopener">Daniel Kehlmann</a> of acting, and this is not meant to be a compliment!), but you know what? Just read the book. It is a true classic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15086936633687232835" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2723578530" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2723578530" />
    <a title="Jonfaith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith"><img alt="Jonfaith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534772484p2/2327886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2723578530?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonfaith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonfaith" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith">Jonfaith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2723578530" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4616471603804825510"><b>They had failed to understand that there was no such thing as private life in wartime Germany. No amount of reticence could change the fact that every individual German belonged to the generality of Germans and must share in the general destiny of Germany, even as more and more bombs were falling on the just and unjust alike.</b><br/><br/><br/>I found this novel to be incredibly moving. It did lag in the middle when the attention drifts from the grieved couple to myriad shitbags. I found the development of the cit</span>
  <span id="freeText4616471603804825510" style="display:none"><b>They had failed to understand that there was no such thing as private life in wartime Germany. No amount of reticence could change the fact that every individual German belonged to the generality of Germans and must share in the general destiny of Germany, even as more and more bombs were falling on the just and unjust alike.</b><br /><br /><br />I found this novel to be incredibly moving. It did lag in the middle when the attention drifts from the grieved couple to myriad shitbags. I found the development of the city's underbelly a bit of a distraction but one very necessary ultimately to the larger context. I tend to agree with the critical response that Fallada succeeded in capturing the atmosphere of totalitarianism. <br /><br /><br />A quiet couple in 1940 Berlin learn of their son's being killed in action. These quiet people decide that action is imperative. Fallada based the novel on some real events and it has recently been adapted into an English-language film. I was aware of the novel's narrative arc but found myself needing the long-form revelation. I was impressed with the psychology revealed as well as the decision to be vivid. This isn't for the squeamish or the sentimental.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4616471603804825510" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_49258670" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49258670" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1473731-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1219988864p2/1473731.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49258670?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1473731-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49258670" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7260818468192896605">Hans Fallada is the pen name of German writer Rudolf Ditzen. Starting his writing career in the 1920s, Fallada continued to write through the fall of the Weimar Republic, the Great Depression, and the rise of the Nazi Party to its rule of Germany. He stayed in Germany after the Nazis took power and managed to survive the war becoming an author of some note in Soviet-occupied Germany after the war.<br/><br/><i>Every Man Dies Alone</i> looks at one couple's small act of resistance to the Nazis during the war. At t</span>
  <span id="freeText7260818468192896605" style="display:none">Hans Fallada is the pen name of German writer Rudolf Ditzen. Starting his writing career in the 1920s, Fallada continued to write through the fall of the Weimar Republic, the Great Depression, and the rise of the Nazi Party to its rule of Germany. He stayed in Germany after the Nazis took power and managed to survive the war becoming an author of some note in Soviet-occupied Germany after the war.<br /><br /><i>Every Man Dies Alone</i> looks at one couple's small act of resistance to the Nazis during the war. At the same time, Fallada creates a detailed depiction of life in Berlin in the early 40s and at the time of the most success of the Third Reich. <i>Every Man Dies Alone</i> is based on the true story of Elise and Otto Hampel, a normal, working class couple who wrote and left numerous anonymous anti-Nazi cards and letters around Berlin. For this small act of resistance, they were hunted by the Berlin Police and the Gestapo. After their capture and "trial" before the infamous Judge Roland Freisler, President of the <i>Volksgericht</i> and representative of the Ministry of Justice at the <i>Wahnsee Konferenz</i>, they were executed for treason. Fallada takes this history and tells the story of his characters, Anna and Otto Quangel, who, after the death of their only son combat in France, take up a similar campaign of resistance. There are numerous other well-drawn character such as Inspector Escherich, the Gestapo detective who lead the hunt for the Quangels and who himself is brutalized by the Gestapo and the SS for his failure to quickly bring the case to a successful conclusion. There are a number of other characters who resist the Nazi regime in their own small, and largely unsuccessful ways. Fallada portrays the brutality of the Nazi regime and its members in detail, showing how the Party worked to dehumanize its members to lead lives of unthinking, fearful obedience.<br /><br />In the face of this, the small acts of resistance seem almost senseless given that they could have no chance of a meaningful effect on overturning or even significantly disrupting the systems created by the Nazis or the war effort itself.<br /><br />But <i>Every Man Dies Alone</i> is not a book about that. It is a book about how an individual maintains his own humanity and character in the face of such a regime. The answer it gives is that you maintain by just resisting, by not cooperating. One of the most important lines in the book, I think, is this. After being told that by her husband that his plot is to just leave anonymous, subversive postcards around Berlin, Anna Quangel is disappointed that the scheme, for which they're likely to be executed is not grander or more remarkable. She then comes to this realization:<br /><br /><blockquote>He might be right: whether their act was big or small, no one could risk more than his life. Each according to his strength and abilities, but the main thing was, you fought back.</blockquote><br /><br />This is a powerful line of thought.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7260818468192896605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CsP662+uKlnyYq97sogVz7cBZnsn1XBQnlnVI/UNnHfKBUokaCfxBULfvE9GxqfS9za9EZcdLjclCJgRK0HHJw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FGnMFeFygMXvKY/B6bRbNA+gaRd9g5RgKSsDoFGZL3nUr3za5vtbmV+UnPUd+ukpT5eyfc1LygeSek6Sj9V0KQ==')})
  }
